There are 5 times more bikes than cars in 
the City Copenhagen 
For every kilometer travelled by bicycle, 
society gains approx. 0,8€ in socioeconomic 
benefits. In Capital Region means annual 
savings of approx. 1€ billion.
Adults who bike to work or use the bike 
every purposes have 30% lower morbidity 
than comparable persons.
Children who bike to school are able to 
focus for 4 hours more every day than chil-
dren who arrive by car.

==PAGE:177==
Biologically Inspired Design
2
Methods & materials
1
Introduction
Nowadays, anyone can ﬁnd a great variety of high-quality raincoats from expert outdoor brands
such as Helly Hansen, The North Face or Rab, most of which use Gore-Tex technology to produce
jackets that are both waterproof and breathable. However, the materials used in the fabrics are
not very sustainable, as they are often made with hazardous processed polymers. Additionally, the
breathable function is usually not as eﬀective when exercising.
The aim of this project is to ﬁnd an alternative sustainable solution for a waterproof and
breathable textile by taking inspiration from nature.
It is going to be based on the functional
challenge: one way humidity transport.
As mentioned before, the main objective is to create a textile that keeps out water but al-
lows for humidity to escape from the inside, by simultaneously demonstrating water tightness and
breathability. In order to scope down the problem and deﬁne it, various exercises were carried out,
designed to help establish the goals and ambitions of the problem at hand.
After that was completed, it was decided that the main requirements that the ﬁnal product
should fulﬁll was to allow movement, water transport and to regulate temperature. By using breath-
able, waterproof, ﬂexible and lightweight materials, the clothing would be able to: manage sweat,
reduce layers of clothing and prevent feeling cold after exercising. After the functions had been
deﬁned, a hypothesis was formed: a raincoat can be made more breathable by drawing inspiration
from nature than by using existing solutions.
2
Methods & materials
2.1
Challenge analysis
The concept that the team decided on was to look at the one-way humidity transport aspect
in raincoats. Following that, the next step was to establish the main challenges associated with the
scope of the project. The recommended way of formulating this was to sketch a functional tree, as
seen in Figure A.1. This allowed a clear overview of the obstacles that the team needed to account
for and ﬁgure out.
A four-box analysis, as seen in Figure A.2, was subsequently executed to allow for a broader
and more complete problem description. This exercise is meant to deﬁne the goals and targets of
the project that the team should strive for.
2.2
Formulation of keywords
In order to connect the challenges and goals to biological inspirations, a keyword-formulation
exercise was completed [1]. These keywords were kept as general as possible in order to ﬁnd the
most potential biological analogies. This formulation can be seen in Figure A.3. These keywords
were then connected to search terms from the biomimicry taxonomy, seen in Figure A.4, that had
been supplied by the course. This connected the challenges to biological phrases that would yield
more results on AskNature.
1
Biologically Inspired Design
2
Methods & materials
2.3
Bio-research
The team split up the relevant search terms and did individual research on each function, which
was then documented in a shared document. This was mostly carried out using AskNature. An
example of the documentation can be seen in Figure A.5. Further research was also carried out
on scientiﬁc literature sites such as Science Direct, Semantic Scholar, DTU FindIt and Web Of
Science. As part of the course, the team also visited the zoo, in hopes to be inspired by seeing the
biological strategies up close. Finally, with all the research done, the biological analogies with the
most promising applications were made into "biocards"[2].
Biocards, seen in Figures B.1-B.11, are a template used to document biological phenomena
that could be employed to solve a problem that has yet to be worked out or to solve the problem
in a better way than the existing solutions. These cards had to include the following:
• Title
– The title should sum up the biological analogy and the function it serves.
• Picture
– A photograph or illustration of the biological organism/phenomenon.
• Function
– Should describe the problem that needs to be solved.
• Biomimicry function
– The function taken from the biomimicry taxonomy.
• Biological phenomenon
– A short description of the biological organism and how it performs the function.
• Functional principle
– An abstract explanation of how the biological mechanism works, along with an illustrative
depiction of the principle.
As will be discussed in the following chapter, the biological phenomenon that the team chose
to try to implement was that of the pine cone [3]. The pine cone utilizes a bilayer mechanism [4] to
respond to environmental humidity by changing its shape, as can be seen in Figure C.1.
2.4
Conceptualization
An ideation exercise called "brainwalking" was performed, in which the team thought up several
elements to consider in connection with breathability. Each team member had 3 minutes to sketch
or explain what they came up with relating to that aspect and when the 3 minutes were up, the
papers were handed to the person on the left and the clock started again. This allowed the team to
see what the others were thinking and also come up with ideas and concepts based on the others’
thoughts.
A concept was born from this exercise: A structure on a raincoat that changes its shape due
to humidity, thus adjusting air ﬂow based on the user’s heat and sweat.
The team ﬁlled in a prototyping planner to deﬁne and validate the experiments, seen in Figure
A.6 and A.7. The next step was to ﬁnd a material that deforms or alters its anatomy when in contact
with water/humidity. Several surfaces were produced, all layered with a rubber-like, waterproof
fabric. They can be viewed in Figures C.2 and C.3. The surfaces were picked or designed to expand
with the absorption of moisture, while the rubber-like fabric was to act as stiﬀﬁbers to guide the
ﬂex direction of the multi-layered surface.
2
==PAGE:178==
Biologically Inspired Design
3
Results
The experimental hypothesis was that the surfaces the team designed would deform and change
their shape when exposed to water or humidity. In the ﬁrst experiment the surfaces were hung on
one end and sprayed with water to see if the materials reacted at all to the liquid. The second
experiment was to expose the materials to humidity. To make this possible, the materials were
placed in a large, closed plastic bag along with a humidiﬁer. The humidity in this closed space was
allowed to reach 94% and the materials kept at that humidity for around 15 minutes.
3
Results
3.1
Four-box analysis
Several methods were used during the problem deﬁnition, such as the four-box analysis that
helped us get an overview of the challenge.
Figure 3.1: Four-box analysis
As stated before, this gives a very clear overview of the problems and challenges.
3.2
Formulation of search terms
The keywords and search terms found using the formulation exercise were divided into three
categories:
• Waterproof: Water Repellent, Hydrophobic, Hydrophilic, Cell Membrane, Impermeable,
Boundary Layer, Protect from water and wind, Non Polar compound.
• Transport: Exchange, Transport, Transfer, One way valve, Transpiration Stream, Cold-heat
Interface, Directionality of water droplets, Transpiration, Valve.
• Breathable: Moisture Management, Permeable Surface, Water Evaporation, Filter, Gas
Exchange, Sweat, Permeability.
3
Biologically Inspired Design
4
Discussion
3.3
Biological phenomena
The following list details the biological phenomena deemed to be the most applicable to the
challenge:
Animals:
• One way ﬂow of blood,
• Wasp saliva to create waterproof nest,
• Dromedary Camel’s nasal surface captures
water from air,
• Sweating aids thermoregulation through
evaporative cooling,
• Otters Fur keeps heat in and cold water
out.
Plants:
• Lotus leaf super-hydrophobic,
• Hierarchical pore networks,
• Cabbage leaves superhydrophobic,
• Leaf cuticle allows select chemicals to pass.
==PAGE:179==
Biologically Inspired Design
5
Conclusion
weight and reduced ﬂexibility. If this were to actually go into production for retail markets, an-
other ﬂexing material would be needed as wood generally isn’t a feasible solution for garments.
A synthetic, hygroscopic layer would be preferable to be durable enough while still performing its
function. This would still be done with consideration to the sustainability factor, it is the team’s
commitment to make sure that the ﬁnal product has a lesser impact on the environment than the
existing solutions. Nature itself often sets the example of maintaining balance in an ecosystem.
Therefore, bio-inspired designs signify a great opportunity to consider sustainability as a leading
criteria for delivering innovative solutions. In line with the circularity of nature, the Sustainable
Development Goals (SDGs) were used as a north star in the process. Two SDGs were selected:
• SDG 9 Industry, Innovation and Infrastructure:
– This goal aims to build resilient infrastructures, promote sustainable industrialisation
and foster innovation [5]. This relates to the project, as the main objective was to alter
the outdoor garment industry to make the materials and processes that most brands use
more sustainable.
• SDG 12 Responsible Consumption and Production:
– Unsustainable patterns of production and consumption are among the main causes of
pollution, biodiversity loss and climate change. More in depth, target 12.5 states that
by 2030 it is essential to "substantially reduce waste generation through prevention,
reduction, recycling and reuse"[6].
The team believes that with further material ex-
ploration and experimentation, they can come up with a more environmentally friendly
and durable fabric to implement in raincoats. Perhaps this can be achieved by looking
more into nanostructures such as employed by the cicada and the springtail. As a conse-
quence, the life of the garment could be extended, aiding in a reduction of fabric waste
and chemical processes associated with the clothing industry.
5
Conclusion
The functional principle works and shows potential based on the experiments performed. How-
ever, the application of it in raincoats and fabrics needs further testing, as of now only the principle
has been proven to work. Speciﬁcally, the scale structure, Figure C.10, must be proven to func-
tion in the desired way, keeping all water out even when the scales are ﬂexed open. A diﬀerent
active material would certainly need to be developed, one that is lightweight, durable, ﬂexible and
hygroscopic. Furthermore, once the pattern of scales is designed in detail, it can be made into a
patch with versatile use. It could be applied not only to rain jackets, but to a variety of purposes,
for example on the external layer of camping tents. The solution solves the functional challenge of
one way humidity transport without the need of an external source of energy, but simply by taking
advantage of unutilized humidity.
5
Biologically Inspired Design
